B00D6DVSAE|3.0|The Last Ship is the soundtrack to a forthcoming play of the same name and its origins were formed in Sting's fantastic 1991 release The Soul Cages.  Given it is, in essence, a musical score, it may be unfair to simply review The Last Ship as a Sting solo album. Nonetheless, that what I am going to do, as  I, and I suspect many fans, will purchase this album less as a score and more as a Sting album.<br /><br />Having said that, your reception of The Last Ship is likely to be the result of your expectations - If you're expecting  80s Sting (i.e., Dream of the Blue Turtles, Nothing Like the Sun), you will certainly be disappointed.  There are a few songs consistent with some 90s Sting (Brand New Day, Ten Summoner's Tales, Soul Cages, Mercury Falling), but even then, you may be left wanting. Not surprisingly, The Last Ship, is most consistent with latter day eclectic Sting.<br /><br /> The best tracks, such as the excellent \\"And Yet,\\" \\"Practical Arrangement,\\" and \\"I Love Her But She Loves Someone Else\\" could have come from nearly any point in Sting's career.  Overall, however, the album is alienating, varying, as it does, between traditional English folk and shanty songs (\\"Language of Birds,\\" \\"Ballad of the Great Eastern\\") and more theatrical songs  (such as \\"The Night the Pugilist Learned to Dance;\\" my least favorite track, \\"What Have We Got;\\" and \\"So to Speak\\"). Sting's choice to deliver some of the songs in a Geordie accent does not help to make the songs any more accessible to fans of his older music. The bonus tracks on the Deluxe Editions are generally of the more theatrical nature, which is not surprising given the fact they were drawn from the reportedly more than 40 tunes available for the stage version of The Last Ship.<br /><br />Regardless of whether or not you like this album, you have to respect Sting's eclectism and nonchalance about the commercialism of his music - certainly even the best tracks on The Last Ship have no chance of radio airplay. Sting has always been an adventurous sort - sometimes his excursions into different genres have produced great stuff (e.g., his jazz influenced Dream of the Blue Turtles and Bring on the Night), but not always (the classically influenced Songs From the Labyrinth).  However, respect doesn't necessarily translate to repeated plays of an album like The Last Ship and if you are a fan of Sting's more traditional rock or jazz influenced work, you're likely to be bored by this album.

0307887898|5.0|Lean Startup is a well-written book with a lot of fresh ideas and thinking about start-ups and entrepreneurship. Eric Ries, author of the book, has had a fair share of start-up experience and in his last start-up (IMVU) experimented with a lot of non-standard ways of managing start-ups influenced by Steve Blank and the Toyota Production System (or lean and hence Lean Startup).<br /><br />The book is divided in 3 parts which roughly map to 'phases' of start-ups: 1) vision, 2) steer, and 3) accelerate. Each of these parts is divided in 4 chapters each simply named with one word such as \\"Test\\" or \\"Measure\\" (not incredibly descriptive).<br /><br />The first part is called Vision and defines Lean Start-up and its influences. It explains how start-ups are about validated learning and how the optimization of start-ups needs to be about how fast they go through the build-measure-learn cycle. When starting a new product, the first thing you need to get is feedback on the product, so it is important to get a Minimum Viable Product out as quickly as possible as learning about how to use the product is a lot more powerful than research or asking customers.<br /><br />The second part continues with the Minimum Viable Product and the learning. The learning we'll need to get from the product is whether our business assumptions (leap-of-faith assumptions) were in fact correct. Start-ups need to change their accounting system to measure their progress in validated learning. When there is enough learning, they can check their leap-of-faith assumption and make the decision to pivot (change a bit in one direction) or persevere (continue).<br /><br />The last part (accelerate) first covers small batches, one of the basic concepts behind lean start-up and then explores the different \\"engine of growth\\" that companies have and stress to focus on one of these. After that it gives Eric's perspective on building lasting companies that continue to improve. The author shared his adaptation of the five why technique. It ends with some general discussion about innovation in larger companies.<br /><br />The epilogue contains a surprisingly positive perspective on the principled of scientific management from Fredrick Taylor.<br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed the book quite a lot, especially the first 2 parts. The way the author has weaved a bunch of concepts together into one holistic framework is quite nice and very concrete. It is not easy to manage your company the way the author suggests as it requires a lot of discipline and introspection (both lacking in many companies). But the Lean Startup moves us away from the black art of startups and creates an interesting structure, or at least perspective, to starting up new ventures. Recommended reading.he five why technique. It ends with some general discussion about innovation in larger companies.<br /><br />The epilogue contains a surprisingly positive perspective on the principled of scientific management from Fredrick Taylor.<br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed the book quite a lot, especially the first 2 parts. The way the author has weaved a bunch of concepts together into one holistic framework is quite nice and very concrete. It is not easy to manage your company the way the author suggests as it requires a lot of discipline and introspection (both lacking in many companies). But the Lean Startup moves us away from the black art of startups and creates an interesting structure, or at least perspective, to starting up new ventures. Recommended reading.

B00EQNYOYU|4.0|&#34;Hesitation Marks&#34; is an album that feels every bit like a NIN album. Trent Reznor doesn't reach for the lofty, ambitious heights of previous albums like The Downward Spiral and The Fragile, but he doesn't offer up cookie-cutter, formulaic industrial tunes either. On each song, you can feel the unique songwriting style, the often infectious melodies, and the excellent production that Reznor has become known for, and you'll find at least a couple of songs that you'll be humming to yourself later.<br /><br />The overall sound feels like a cross between the debut NIN album &#34;Pretty Hate Machine&#34; and the veteran album &#34;With Teeth&#34;. Each song definitely has a mechanical flavor to it, notably in the percussive background and synthesized instruments. In the past twenty-five or so years, however, Reznor has become an expert at layering voices and sounds to create a powerful effect, or a contemplative quietness, or really whatever he wants to convey.<br /><br />In some ways this album sounds less forceful than others, a bit more restrained, and that may not be everyone's cup of tea. Reznor is a bit less aggressive and a bit more melodic in Hesitation Marks than he has been in the past, perhaps drawing influence from his own work with his new band How To Destroy Angels. This album, however, has the same excellent songwriting as any other album he's made in the past ten years, and the familiar and undeniable NIN style. Hesitation Marks acts as an excellent reintroduction to Nine Inch Nails, and hopefully a sign of more great music to come.

Other incidents are similarly detailed.<br /><br />The 'good news' is that there have been no accidental or unauthorized nuclear explosions, we and the Russians now have fewer missiles than before, and undoubtedly safety precautions have improved considerably. B00DJ80VLS|5.0|With headphones secure I sat down to listen to the new Dream Theater album.  In 1992 DT changed my musical landscape forever when I heard Images & Words. Illusions broken.<br /><br />Now, 24 years since WDADU came out, I clicked play on 2013's Dream Theater.  This album feels like the band hit the reset button.  Don't get me wrong, all of the things I love about DT are here.  The crunchy and soaring guitar of John Petrucci, the unstoppable grove of John Myung, the cinematic and morphing wizardry of Jordan Rudess and his many keyboard and iPad apps, the almost robotic and precise drumming of \\"the new guy\\" Mike Mangini and on top of that the soulful and elegant vocals of James Labrie.  Sometimes his voice can turn people off, similar to Geddy Lee in irritation of Rush, but James delivers on this disc.<br /><br />I say reset as this is not a continuation of the last album.  No, this sounds like the guys all sat in the studio together, talked about what they each wanted from this effort and worked to achieve it.  Gone are some of the long interludes on previous outings.  I love it when DT jam for 5 or 10 minutes showcasing the talents of these amazing musicians but this album seems more refined in that area.  DT is sometimes criticized for over the top bombast vs. thought out songwriting.  I would say they have the perfect balance here.<br /><br />I won't break down each song here, many others will take care of that.  What I will do is say the band I discovered so long ago is alive and well in the 21st century.  A seemingly renewed sense of purpose.  I write this on the day the album came out and after just one listen, I can say this is my album of the year.  I look forward to many more listens and looking forward to enjoying this delicious piece of chocolate cake.ly renewed sense of purpose. 0385743564|3.0|I consider Brandon Sanderson one of my very favorite authors. Anything he writes automatically goes on my wishlist, and I've enjoyed every book of his I've read. With Steelheart, however, there was a time where I wasn't even sure if I would be able to rate this book three stars at the end. This book doesn't live up to the other Sanderson novels I've read, the first in three other series (The Stormlight Archive, Alcatraz, and Mistborn). Of course, I'm sure different sorts of readers may like this one better, but I'm left rather disappointed, probably due to my exceedingly high expectations.<br /><br />As ever, where Brandon Sanderson shines is in the world building. Sanderson has a real knack for creating unique worlds that run on different principles, following that logic through. This world, while not as disparate as his epic fantasies, compels the imagination. Superhero stories have always held a fascination for me, and, with the Epics, Sanderson has done something wholly new, to my experience anyway, with that concept. The Epics are villains, every last one of them, proving the old adage that absolute power corrupts absolutely.<br /><br />That premise may suggest to you, and rightly so, that Steelheart gets rather dark. This is not a world of brave, unparalleled heroism, of good triumphing over evil. Even the good guys have dark secrets and motivations. Take, for example, the main character, David. He's a fairly average teen, except for that whole thing where he's dedicated his life to avenging his father, with the single-minded devotion of Inigo Montoya.<br /><br />Steelheart, the titular villain, rules over a Newcago, a Chicago made of steel where the sun literally doesn't shine. Steelheart and his cronies, including Nightwielder who blocks the sun from the city, keep systems going and food available to the people, in exchange for utter domination. Epics, these super-powered individuals, can do anything to any person without recrimination; the government of the US limps along in some areas of the Fractured States, but the Epics truly have free reign. It's a horrific picture, rather like humanity has begun involving and Magneto's crew have subjugated everyone, and there are no X-Men.<br /><br />Sanderson also proves himself a skilled plotter once again. In every novel, Sanderson always manages to surprise me with something, if not with an actual twist then with exactly how something worked, even if I had it generally figured out. That always makes his books that extra bit of exciting to read.<br /><br />However, the character-building in Steelheart really left me wanting so much more. In his other novels, I've found his characters almost immediately compelling, not just the main characters, but the supporting ones as well. With Steelheart, I didn't much care for a single character. The Reckoners, a team of humans killing Epics to prove it can be done and that fighting back is possible, should be a quirky bunch with all the personality of the crew of the Firefly. Sadly, though they're quirky, none of them really coalesced into feeling real and their witty banter was seriously lacking.<br /><br />Many of the problems lie in the narrator, David. He alternates between an encyclopedic knowledge of weaponry and Epics and mindless devotion to his cause of killing Steelheart and drooling over Megan. He instaloves all over the place on this girl because she's super hot and can fight. Wow, what a solid foundation for a relationship. As far as I can tell, Sanderson's pushing this ship, but he's got a lot of work to do to make that happen, because they have no chemistry: David's a kid with pathetic puppy love and Megan's...I don't really know.<br /><br />Anyway, David. Not only do I not found David to be a particularly interesting character, his narration is frequently annoying. Idiosyncracies help build realistic characters, but, in this case, I feel like David was given one idiosyncracy and sent on his way like the work was done. That idiosyncracy: making really ridiculous metaphors constantly, like:<br /><br />\\"He had a smile like a parrotfish, which I've always assumed look like parrots, though I've never actually seen either.\\"<br /><br />What incredibly useful narration there, David! You have just managed to tell the reader nothing on several levels. Congratulations! There are many such instances, and it's made into a running gag throughout the book, but it's one that failed to make me laugh; the only response it provoked from me was eye-rolling. I can imagine this working from a character who isn't otherwise so deadly serious, but it didn't fit the rest of David's characterization.<br /><br />Then there's the Southern Scot, who embodies every stereotype of American Southerners and the Scottish, down to a monologue on the bagpipes that is TOTALLY plot-relevant (SARCASM). This guy, Cody, threw me out of the book every time he referred to individuals as \\"y'all\\" and groups as \\"all y'all.\\" I've been informed that some southerners do use \\"y'all\\" this way, but it makes my eye twitch because \\"y'all\\" is an abbreviation of \\"YOU ALL.\\" IT IS PLURAL. Also, why is this necessary? As with David's quirk, I feel like the weaknesses of the characterization are largely shortcuts taken to add humor to the novel and make them seem more real and quirky. Sadly, this backfired big time.<br /><br />Though this is thus far my least favorite of Sanderson's works, he can still world build like few other authors. The Reckoners series will be a good choice for fans of supervillains and world building. More character-focused readers, like myself, may struggle with this one.at idiosyncracy: making really ridiculous metaphors constantly, like:<br /><br />\\"He had a smile like a parrotfish, which I've always assumed look like parrots, though I've never actually seen either.\\"<br /><br />What incredibly useful narration there, David! You have just managed to tell the reader nothing on several levels. Congratulations! There are many such instances, and it's made into a running gag throughout the book, but it's one that failed to make me laugh; the only response it provoked from me was eye-rolling. I can imagine this working from a character who isn't otherwise so deadly serious, but it didn't fit the rest of David's characterization.<br /><br />Then there's the Southern Scot, who embodies every stereotype of American Southerners and the Scottish, down to a monologue on the bagpipes that is TOTALLY plot-relevant (SARCASM). This guy, Cody, threw me out of the book every time he referred to individuals as \\"y'all\\" and groups as \\"all y'all.\\" I've been informed that some southerners do use \\"y'all\\" this way, but it makes my eye twitch because \\"y'all\\" is an abbreviation of \\"YOU ALL.\\" IT IS PLURAL. Also, why is this necessary? As with David's quirk, I feel like the weaknesses of the characterization are largely shortcuts taken to add humor to the novel and make them seem more real and quirky. The Reckoners series will be a good choice for fans of supervillains and world building. More character-focused readers, like myself, may struggle with this one.

1250030951|5.0|Calling all Muggles, Mundanes, Twilighters, Trekkies, Jedis, Hobbits, and... [insert your fandom here]: Rainbow Rowell has written a book with YOUR name on it... and it is called FANGIRL. (Or you can mentally rename it FANBOY, if that feels more appropriate).<br /><br />Not really being one who has ever fallen so far into a fandom beyond attending midnight launch parties for the final three Harry Potter novels (sans costume, the final book while pregnant), even I found myself connecting with the main character, Cath, and her love for all things Simon Snow. Although I have never been one to write fan fiction, I have loved something enough to draw fan art on the occasion. Perhaps those are (arguably) almost the same thing? Regardless, I've never seriously obsessed over a franchise... If you have been there and you have done that when it comes to extreme fandom and fan fiction, FANGIRL may very well be your gospel.<br /><br />Beyond the basic premise, as well as the incredibly creative Simon Snow \\"fan fiction\\" excerpts staggered throughout the novel, FANGIRL also involves a much deeper story. FANGIRL is as much about family, friends, growing up and discovering oneself as it is about the wonderful world of fanaticism. It's also about building trust, trust in yourself and trust in others. Cath has been burned in her life; she has a mother who left her and a father who may or may not be falling apart mentally. Many kids experience some sort of \\"life crisis\\" when it comes time to leave for college. Cath's life crisis has arrived at full force, and she's not quite sure if she's ready to face it.<br /><br />A bit of an anti-social internet sensation, Cath is an online fan fiction writer who has never really done anything for herself. Well, sure her love for Simon Snow and every action she takes as a fan of the series is kind of like \\"doing something for herself\\" - since being a fan is what she wants to do... But she's always had her sister by her side when it comes to school, and at home she's only ever thought of her father. Remove the father figure and remove the sister, and what does Cath have left? What do any of us actually \\"have\\" when we first venture out into the world and must give some thought to our futures? The exploration of many \\"life altering\\" questions are handled brilliantly in Rainbow's FANGIRL.<br /><br />There's a bit of romance involving a young college boy who will make you want to simultaneously pinch as well as slap his cheeks. Here, too, is a kid who needs to figure out his priorities in life. I found the growth of the character, Levi, to be just as engrossing as that of the growth of Cath (or as he calls her, \\"Cather\\"). Ugh, but if he didn't make one or two mistakes that seriously made me want to wring his neck... Thank goodness he redeemed himself and was just plain adorable.<br /><br />Although it gets a bit choppy at times when it comes to the progression of time over the duration of the school year and, subsequently, the novel, FANGIRL will hook the reader from the very beginning and not let go until the end. You will cheer for Cath, want to hold her hand at times, fall in love with her writing (which has an ironic twist to it in the end) and become proud of her as she grows. It's worth mentioning that FANGIRL has a very polished and wrapped up ending. B00DJ80VLS|3.0|Upon my initial listening to this album it was tough to gauge if this is a collection of songs which would stay with me over time. I look at Dream Theater as everything before 'Train of Thought' (An album that i love) and everything after 'Train of Thought'. From 'Octavarium' onward, the band's songs seem to have often been plagued by riffs that hang on too long, bad lyrics, and a lack of feeling. This capped itself off with what could easily be the bands worst album in 'Black Clouds' and the resulting departure of Mike Portnoy. To their credit however, each album had a couple of great tracks, and Systematic Chaos in particular was very strong throughout.    With this, the bands newest and self titled Album, i still long for the raw passionate riffs of old. There is nothing here that remotely resembles the deeply authentic sounds of any song from their masterpiece 'Awake' album, nor does it include any of the wild time signatures and tempo changes of 'Scenes from a Memory'. I still long for the muddy emotion of 'Misunderstood', or a story as real as 'Take away my pain' or 'Scarred'. One thing in particular that has bothered me in recent years with Dream Theater's lyrics is that they have descended into the vague, or at times simply a story without much impact. Their older works have a much sharper edge that way, and it always bled into the music. The reality is, if you were to hear the Awake demos from 1994 and hear the level of raw passion in '6:00' and 'Caught in a Web' from those tapes, or the live stuff from 1997, and then listen to this, it is simply several levels below.    Perhaps it is just the natural process which most bands fall to over the course of time. It is difficult to find the raw passion of the first time forever. If you take a look at old videos from the bands 'Images' or 'Work in Progress' tours, you see real stage energy. As the years wear on, their movements become less and less until eventually it is gone almost completely. So then, here is acollection of very good songs, but none of which really have the raw strength of what came before.    At this point it may seem that this is then a bad album, but that is not the case. This new offering from Dream Theater is without doubt, strong as anything they have put out in recent years. There is less tedium in the riffs than 'Constant Motion', as the chord progressions are generally bright and exciting. Another real high point is the bass work by Myung, which is arguably among the best of his career. Technically the music is sound. The guitar work is especially crisp with good differentiation between sound and silence. It never falls into that noodling trap of cramming too many notes into a small space. There is character to the solos, although they never really reach the melodic heights of 'Take the Time'. James in particular has been growing ever stronger on vocals in recent years. While he has still never, and may never again reach the pinnacle of his power as he did in 1997, the most recent 'Dramatic Turn of Events' tour saw him at his best since 2004, and probably a little bit stronger still. This renaissance in James' vocals shows in the album work. It is strong throughout.    To further expand upon this point in a new paragraph i am writing now a year after my original review, the trend of better live performance has continued throughout 2014. James is undeniably delivering some of the better performances of his career lately. I find that Mangini has a good skill for holding the drum tempo down slightly, giving Petrucci the time to crisply land his notes. Whatever the opinion on the 'better overall' drummer is not an argument i intend to make here. Simply it seems that Mangini does an excellent job of defining the down beat. The trickle down effect has empowered Petrucci, but it would not be unreasonable to think that it is also empowering James.     The album mix is generally strong, although perhaps a bit louder than i would prefer. I am personally of mind that a good sound system can make the music loud. Also, most users will add their own EQ to the mix, and so the master should peak below the threshold. Still, it is far from the muddled mess that turned one of Dream Theater's finest collections of songs (Falling into Infinity) into a product that sounded as though it was by far their worst. The final product represents them very well, and the song structure translates extremely well onto stage.    There is not a weak track on this album, although 'The Looking Glass' and 'Enigma Machine' are easily the best, both elevating to a slightly higher level than the rest. Thankfully the band has chosen to perform both on stage and they sound great. 'Illumination Theory' is one that never grabbed me on the album, but i have come to appreciate it more in show. It is still not decidedly the best of their 20 minute songs though. I wish that we could hear more of this album live, in particular 'Surrender to Reason'. Trying to find a proper review score for this album on the whole is difficult. The swath of 5 star ratings to come is expected and understandable. Then, on the other hand, I am certain that there is no song in this collection that is even as remotely as brilliant as 'Scarred', 'Learning to Live', 'Take away my pain', 'Ones who help to set the sun', the whole 'Six Degrees' album, or 'Fatal Tragedy' among so many others. There is simply nothing here that reaches that level of songwriting. The longer i let this album sit in my mind, which is now well over a year, the more it fades away from my memory. Their stage performance in the years 2013/2014 do not though. It is worth making that important distinction.    This collection is worth investing in, and as a singer, i crave an instrumental copy to sing on. It just leaves me wanting so much more. It is completely sound on a technical level, but is missing songs that evolve and morph into something greater, when the rawness of a story like 'Scarred' fuels the music.It would be near impossible to simply walk out the front door of your house in the morning and make that story happen, and perhaps in that way, Dream Theater is damned. As a long time fan however, i hope that this competent release is just another stepping stone in an upward trend. I also love how Mangini's particular talents have changed the band. This is a band that feels as though it is on the way up as opposed to down. It is simply that until the final barrier is breached beyond technical excellence to raw emotion, their new studio cuts will continue to fade from mind. Therefore, the final verdict is three stars out of five.

B00DJZR46G|4.0|To listen to Elton John on \\"The Diving Board\\" is almost like listening to Elton Verite.  Producer T-Bone Burnett, as he and Elton were finishing up work on the Elton/Leon Russell collaboration \\"The Union,\\" reminded Elton that the first time Burnett saw him play, it was the legendary lives shows on Elton's first visit to Los Angeles. As a trio, Elton, a bassist and a drummer. Why, Burnette asked, have you never recorded in the fashion of those early lives shows, where you drew everyone's attentions as musicians?<br /><br />That is the seed of \\"The Diving Board.\\"  With just drums and bass, most of the songs are built on Elton's piano figures. With a touch of guitars here, a backup choir there, but mostly just the core players, Elton crafts some of the stateliest songs since his debut or \\"Tumbleweed Connection.\\"  Some may mistake the seriousness of the music for Elton being somber, but titles like \\"Can't Stay Alone Tonight,\\" \\"Home Again\\" or the smoky title song prove to be anything but.<br /><br />\\"I Hung out with the old folks, in the hope that I'd get wise,\\" Elton sings at the beginning of \\"The Diving Board's\\" \\"Oceans Away,\\" about the families he and Bernie grew up around following WWII. It's just Elton and his keyboards for the beginning of the album. It brings the focus all back on Elton and that piano. There are even three instrumental interludes to let Elton stretch out between a few of the songs to remind listeners just how much of a talent Elton has been, before and after the outfits and crazy glasses. He remains an exceptional singer and instrumentalist.<br /><br />That is, as has been for many years (excluding a few breaks) with the invaluable assistance of lyricist Bernie Taupin. B00026WU9Q|5.0|It was during the Newport Folk Festival that Bob Dylan brought the electric guitar into his music and it was during the recording of his album \\"Bringing It All Back Home\\" that he brought it into the recording studio. It was a very controversial move in Dylan's career that he moved away from folk music into rock, really folk rock, and many fans were upset by the move. To me it seemed like a natural move artistically which would lead to some of his most creative music. Case in point would be the album \\"Bringing It All Back Home\\" which finds Dylan playing a collection of songs that some would be classified as folk and the rest as rock. It is a collection that not only totally works but would become one of Dylan's best albums created during his career. The first part of the album is the more rock sounding part with songs like \\"Subterranean Homesick Blues\\" to \\"Maggie's Farm\\" to \\"Outlaw Blues\\" among the better ones though they are all good. The second part of the record finds Dylan going back to his folk beginnings with just him, his guitar and harmonica. \\"Mr. Tambourine Man\\", \\"Gates Of Eden\\" and \\"It's Alright Ma (I'm Only Bleeding)\\" are not only some of the best songs here but I think represents some of the absolutely best song lyrics Dylan has ever written especially \\"It's Alright Ma\\" with some of the most vitriolic lyrics written since \\"Masters Of War\\". All in all this is like two albums in one with the two different sounds and feel to the songs. Then again this album came out as Dylan was bringing a change to his music going from acoustic to electric and it seems fitting that \\"Bringing It All Back Home\\" have the combination of the two sounds on it as he was making the transition. This is certainly an album anyone who calls themselves a fan of Dylan has to have in their collection. Even for the most casual fan this is a must have record.

If you're going to read the series, it just comes with the territory. Accepting this, I still can't get past the feeling that he wastes a lot of the reader's time with chapters that ultimately prove to be unimportant (which is why I found myself skimming over a good bit of AFFC). But hey, if you're writing a money-making series, wouldn't you want to drag out the story over as many books as possible? Then suddenly he is a kinslayer who wants to murder a few other relatives and is riddled with wretched thoughts. It's like he turned into Cersei.<br /><br />It was nice to get back in touch with some characters, and there was at least a mention of Rickon, who seemed to have fallen off the face of planet. I found myself wondering, `What's the main idea of this series?' The answer seems to be: Rule Westeros (though with the way things are going, it won't be much of a prize when all is said and done). The first book endears us to the Starks, so we're going to root for them, right? Yet their position in the kingdom, as it stands now, is tenuous at best. Has Daenerys become the character we are now supposed to hope `wins'? At this point, I'm not sure what outcome I'm supposed to be hoping for based on how Martin has set up and developed the story. And that's one of my big problems with him as a writer, at least with regard to `A Song of Ice and Fire'.<br /><br />The series is interesting enough to keep me reading, though if we have another volume like AFFC I'll be pretty wroth. I acknowledge why he broke up the last two books the way he did, but I'm not sure it was the best story-telling strategy. Also, if you have to explain the screwed up timeline before a reader dives in, then perhaps you might want to re-think things. Plus it would move the story along more seamlessly if we didn't have to keep jumping back and forth in time, and we wouldn't have to wait as long to see what our favorite characters are up to. There's no telling how he will choose to divvy up the story lines in the next book, but here's hoping it is a more blended effort.(less) [edit]

Without spending a lot of screen time on it, the movie provides a realistic look at that often unmentioned aspect of the totality of the conflict; and it also shows that the spouses of the officers have responsibilities that in many ways mimic the responsibilities of the officers themselves.<br /><br />A final comment on realistic elements -- ceremony is an important part of military life.  The ceremonies (of several types) are only briefly depicted, and not all of them involve more than two people; but again even the brief clips provide the viewer a sense of what a soldier (or sailor, Marine, airman) experience in the totality of preparing for and heading off to the war-fighting effort.
B00EF1PKJQ|4.0|Sarah's 3 albums are evenly-spaced 2 years apart (2009; 2011; 2013) and show remarkable progress from one to the next, and none more so than between #2 Follow Me Down and this latest release, recorded during her final semester at the New England Conservatory. Her foundation in acoustic bluegrass roots that was so prominent in her debut Song Up in Her Head four years ago is more of a distant echo of the past on Build Me Up From Bones. The songwriting is more sophisticated, the lyrical content more mature and personal, the singing more polished, and the music composition much more adventurous, diving into eclectic blends of folk, jazz, love ballads and even a light touch of neo-psychedelia.<br /><br /> Sarah made her name as a young mandolin prodigy and astounding vocalist with a musical sensibility far beyond her years. On this collection of 9 originals and 2 covers, she fulfills the early promise of her teenage years. The \\"bluegrass wunderkind\\" label has been relegated to the attic trunk - this is a fully-realized work of a grown woman with an old soul and 4 years of advanced musical education at a prestigious school that is tailored to help nurture geniuses in the field. By all accounts, NEC has served Sarah well. She has left her proverbial \\"comfort zone\\" behind.<br /><br />  A word of caution: people expecting a bare-bones, stripped-down rustic sound centered around a banjo or mandolin (akin to her debut record) will get fewer examples of that on this record. Sarah's mando is featured here on just 1 track (Joanna Newsom's 'Book of Right On') and her clawhammer banjo only on 'Fuel the Fire.' The rest of the album is dominated by her preferred instruments: the Fletcher Brock octave-mandolin, and her acoustic guitar. Much has been said of the project emphasizing her live concert trio configuration (Alex Hargreaves on fiddle; Nat Smith on cello), and that is true on 6 of the 11 songs. The other 5 make extensive use of session musicians and electric instruments, including Jerry Douglas on Dobro, Kenny Malone on percussion, Chris Thile on mandolin and Dan Dugmore on pedal steel and electric guitars. Also, as on her 2 other albums, guest vocalists appear on harmony, as well as overdubbed multi-tracks by Sarah herself. With the exception of the Dylan cover 'Simple Twist of Fate' (using only Sarah's voice and Nat's cello plucked like a bass), the album makes good use of the studio's tools to flesh-out the songs beyond the minimalist trio setup. Producer Gary Paczosa even indulges in employing audio special-FX on some tracks like 'Fuel the Fire' and 'Rearrange the Art' that couldn't be reproduced live without a soundboard equipped with ProTools. In fact, the latter track, which closes out the album, has a slightly late-60's psychedelic vibe coupled with jazzy Bela Fleck banjo stylings that showcases how far Jarosz's musical palette has expanded since 2009. If her sophomore album Follow Me Down was her Rubber Soul, this album is her Revolver, with a similar broad range of genres and influences that pulls the listener in to territories with fresh musical visions, showing Sarah's journey is taking her towards new horizons.<br /><br /> Ms. Jarosz is well on her way to establishing herself as a bona-fide original, creating a recognizable, idiosyncratic style drawn from numerous sources and forged into her own unique artistic identity.

0307271609|5.0|This is a vivid and insightful examination of the life and times of the Chinese Dowager Empress Cixi.  Cixi is a much neglected and often maligned ruler; she has often been portrayed as a cruel, ugly caricature of the sinister Asian.  Much of that depiction comes directly from outright lies created by her enemies, some of which was only fairly recently revealed through archived documents.  The real Cixi was a genius who passionately and devotedly lived for her country and her dynasty at an especially turbulent time when the old traditional China was being bombarded by what the Chinese saw as an invasion of Western technology, aggression and greed.<br /><br />How many people today know that the Opium Wars were started by Great Britain to allow them to sell illegal opium from their empire in India to what they regarded as lowly and backward Chinese?  Other countries simply believed they had the right to take over chunks of China as they pleased.  Japan was especially aggressive and belligerent.<br /><br />As the Emperor and his advisors retreated further into the past and tradition, Cixi recognized that burying their heads in the sand would lead to disaster for China.  As a woman and with a rank of fourth concubine, Cixi had no power and no influence.  However, she was not only the mother of the Emperor's only son, but also a dear friend of the Empress, and the two, after the Emperor's death, pulled off a very slick and bloodless coup.  The Empress assumed the administrative chores, while Cixi became responsible for determining policy.<br /><br />Cixi filled her role from behind a screen as a female could not appear in the audience chamber.  She also had to enter the Palace through the back door as females were unworthy of the front door.  (When I visited the Forbidden City, I very much enjoyed the knowledge that I was walking in through the central axis, forbidden to women during the imperial period.)<br /><br />Cixi strove not only to maintain and defend China, but also to find the good that the West had to offer and apply it to China.  She brought technology such as railroads and telegraphs to China, as well as restructuring the education system.  She outlawed foot binding and championed women's rights and women's education.  (In one great scene, Cixi stopped an opera performance over lyrics degrading to women.)  She also planned a literacy campaign that would lead to an informed populace, with the ultimate goal being a constitutional monarchy.<br /><br />Cixi did make mistakes.  As the author, who greatly admires Cixi, states, Cixi was a giant, not a saint.<br /><br />This is a marvelous book.  The author has managed to cover about a hundred years of Chinese history, full of unfamiliar names of people and places, and make it clear, vibrant and absolutely thrilling.  I highly recommend this book to anyone interested in Chinese, world or women's history. B00D1QBQ2G|5.0|Franco Fagioli has been one of the leading countertenors of the new generation for a few years now, ever since winning the prestigious Neue Stimmen competition in Germany in 2003. Recently though, with recordings like [[ASIN:B008R9QAY6 Vinci: Artaserse]] and now [[ASIN:B00DNVWS4Y Steffani: Stabat Mater]] as well as some sensational stage performances, his career has taken a dramatic new trajectory. He used various techniques to unnerve other soloists onstage lest they steal his limelight, and he wasn't averse to challenging those who crossed his path to a duel. (In the London of 1738 however, he met his match in Handel, who was well used to dealing with big egos. B009H75B2E|5.0|The Scottish Dunedin Consort has often been lauded for its fresh, transparent textures and beautifully proportioned and balanced sound, and following critical acclaim for their Baroque vocal releases, such as [[ASIN:B001355OUW Matthew Passion (Final Performing Version, c. 1742)]], [[ASIN:B000K2Q7PK Handel: Messiah (Dublin Version, 1742)]]) and most recently [[ASIN:B009G7WUOM Bach: John Passion]], they've produced what's to be hoped is a curtain-raiser to more recordings of the orchestral works.<br /><br />In the booklet notes John Butt observes that though the six concertos are highly structured, they are, paradoxically 'among the most carefree, joyous and spontaneous works that Bach ever produced'. Butt is able to create broad washes of colour which, combined with an energy generated from within the textural detail, drives the music onward with an effortlessness and inevitability. This fluidity is striking, possibly because many recent recordings of late have instead focused on producing a more rustic and even jagged sound. The flowing elegance here seems to bring out the French influence, and indeed the temperament which the ensemble have adopted is that of the French Court at the time, the low A-392. This in itself has a knock-on effect in slightly slowing the pace and thereby increasing the articulation of the instruments. It also adds a wonderfully warm glow.<br /><br />The idea of the opposing forces of 'disputation' and 'agreement' within the form of the concerto seem to have been a consideration in this recording, and certainly Butt could be said to to have achieved a truly euphonious result. The instrumental colours of the soloists in the Dunedin Consort are beguiling, at times dazzling, but always as part of the larger picture. Altogether the impression is of a fresh insight into works which we all thought we knew so well.

B00EJ2WBX4|5.0|For the past year, buzz about Scottish synthpop band Chvrches has been steadily growing. From appearances at major festivals like SXSW to touring alongside bands like Depeche Mode and Passion Pit, Chvrches have been doing their best to stand out in a sea of synth pop. And on their debut, \\"The Bones of What You Believe\\"--they do in fact stand out in the best of ways. Accessible and fun yet with an underlying aggression and angst paired with strong beats and a full bodied vocal, Chvrches has created an album that many established pop stars don't seem to be capable of making.<br /><br />Lead vocalist Lauren Mayberry deserves much of the credit for the success of this album. A lot of similar bands have girl vocals that are thin and whimsical, but Lauren has a fuller sound, better range and an uncanny skill at building layers of drama and emotion through tiny tone changes. She can casually get away with quite dark lyrics at times and still have the music sound very danceable and light given her sweet clear tone. Just like how it took me several plays of Foster the People's \\"Pumped Up Kicks\\" to actually realize what they were singing about as the lyrics and bouncy tone are quite incongruous, Lauren's music has several similar moments though not to that extreme. Her lyrics have an intelligence and layer of complexity that is unusual, which may or may not be related to the fact that Lauren is highly educated with a full law degree as well as Masters in journalism.<br /><br />Musically, this album doesn't exactly break new ground but when playing the album through--the songs thankfully do stand out from each other which I find is my most common problem with synth based music. It's also more refreshingly straightforward than similar bands like Purity Ring. And yet-with Lauren's vocals--it still has the ability to stomp out major anthems for intense highs. \\"Recover\\" is mind numbingly catchy with a chorus that's going to wreak havoc with your subconscious. \\"Science/Visions\\" has a very ethereal almost movie score quality to it which is very interesting. \\"Gun\\" was just featured on the FIFA 14 soundtrack and suitably shiny and bright.<br /><br />Finally, if you happen to have a chance to see them live--do so. While they are naturally high energy with an infectious enthusiasm---they're also hilarious and self deprecatingly genuine. In short--though not musically brilliant, this album has so much going for it just entices and draws you in. It's like a will-o-the-wisp ghostly shining on a dark moor at night. You just can't help yourself but to follow. And dance.<br /><br />For fans of bands like Passion Pit, M83, The Naked and Famous, Purity Ring and Kate Boy.a very ethereal almost movie score quality to it which is very interesting. \\"Gun\\" was just featured on the FIFA 14 soundtrack and suitably shiny and bright.<br /><br />Finally, if you happen to have a chance to see them live--do so. B00F5BON8O|5.0|It was Sarah's cover of the The Decemberists' \\"Shankill Butchers\\" off of her 2009 debut when she was just 18, that first put her on my radar. Haunting with incredible technical skill on both the banjo and the mandolin--she was incredibly exciting and yet collectively both her debut and follow up record fell flat for me. Quite frankly, it always just felt like she didn't have much to say which in my eyes is one of the worst things in the traditional folk world. It can't just sound good--it needs to be torn from your soul. This is where all that changes.<br /><br />\\"Mile on the Moon\\" has a crooning country atmosphere with an electric guitar giving it a little edge. Album title track \\"Build Me Up from Bones\\" is an early album standout and is a great example of how accessible Sarah can make traditional instruments sound. Soft and lilting with picked cello and fiddle notes, its refreshingly genuine and heartfelt. Sarah's take on Bob Dylan's \\"Simply Twist of Fate\\" is subtle with extremely minimal instrumentation--but sounds like its an original Jarosz song as she so completely owns it. Her other cover, \\"Book of Right-On\\" by Joanna Newsom is actually one of the album's few low points as it doesn't sound that different from the original as well as lacks Joanna's stunning quirk.<br /><br />This album was produced by longtime collaborator, Gary Paczosa (Alison Krauss, Dolly Parton) and also features a few guests spots with Chris Thile, Aoife O'Donovan, and Darrell Scott--but perhaps for the first time--the focus is consistently on Sarah and her band of two, Nathaniel Smith, and Alex Hargreaves. The music sounds more organic like it was all recorded together and less like it was just Sarah's parts with the rest dubbed. Her songwriting maturity is also on full display. Her use of second and third person has been replaced by Sarah finally being an integral part of the story.  And finally, I think the third thing which sets this album apart is the more expansive tone.  In the midst of finishing this album, Sarah graduated with honors from the extremely prestigious New England Conservatory--and I think everything she was exposed to there enabled her to both more more confident in her sound as well as branch out a bit.<br /><br />Sarah is part of a very exciting movement of young people who love traditional folk music yet understand how to frame it within a more modern melodic palette. Highly recommend. For fans of bands/artists like Gillian Welch, Valerie June, Nickel Creek.st of finishing this album, Sarah graduated with honors from the extremely prestigious New England Conservatory--and I think everything she was exposed to there enabled her to both more more confident in her sound as well as branch out a bit.<br /><br />Sarah is part of a very exciting movement of young people who love traditional folk music yet understand how to frame it within a more modern melodic palette.
